201 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Davis v. State


S07A1758.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


283 Ga. 438; 660 S.E.2d 354; 2008 Ga. LEXIS 262; 2008 Fulton County D. Rep. 871



March 17, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied April 14, 2008.
Reconsideration denied by Davis v. State, 2008 Ga. LEXIS 339 (Ga., Apr. 14, 2008)
Stay granted by Davis v. Georgia, 554 U.S. 943, 129 S. Ct. 28, 171 L. Ed. 2d 930, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 5430 (2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Davis v. Georgia, 555 U.S. 967, 129 S. Ct. 397, 172 L. Ed. 2d 323, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 7639 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Habeas corpus. Chatham Superior Court. Before Judge Freesemann.
Davis v. Terry, 465 F.3d 1249, 2006 U.S. App. LEXIS 24290 (11th Cir. Ga., 2006)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a Georgia trial court issued a new order for defendant's execution, he filed an extraordinary motion for new trial, pursuant to O.C.G.A. § 5-5-41(b), which the court denied without a hearing. He then moved for a discretionary appeal, and the State Board of Pardons and Paroles granted a temporary stay of execution. The instant court then granted a discretionary appeal, and the stay was rescinded. Thus, the instant decision followed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's extraordinary motion for new trial relied primarily on affidavit testimony consisting of: recantations by trial witnesses, statements recounting alleged admissions of guilt by a co-defendant, statements that said co-defendant disposed of a handgun following the murder, and an alleged eyewitness account. But, the record evidence failed to support defendant's claim. First, under the right for any reason principle, the affidavits presented lacked the type of materiality required to support a new trial, as they did not show the witnesses's trial testimony to have been the purest fabrication. Further, defendant failed to act diligently in presenting the affidavits he alleged supported his motion. Second, the affidavits supporting the statements recounting a co-defendant's alleged admissions were not sufficiently material. Third, defendant's lack of diligence supported rejection of the statements that the co-defendant disposed of a handgun following the murder. Fourth, at least one original witness never recanted his in-court identification of defendant as the shooter. Finally, defendant failed to present the facts necessary to warrant a hearing on his motion.


OUTCOME: The order denying defendant's extraordinary motion for a new trial was affirmed.

 

202 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Miller v. State


S07A1848.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


283 Ga. 412; 658 S.E.2d 765; 2008 Ga. LEXIS 264; 2008 Fulton County D. Rep. 880



March 17, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied April 11, 2008.
Habeas corpus proceeding at Miller v. Danforth, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 73250 (S.D. Ga., July 7, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Murder. Chatham Superior Court. Before Judge Brannen.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed in part, vacated in part, and remanded with direction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a Chatham County jury convicted defendant of malice murder and related offenses, he appealed, contending that: (1) the trial court erred in admitting the decedent's statements under the necessity exception to the hearsay rule; (2) the trial court erred by giving the pattern jury instruction on prior difficulties evidence; (3) he received ineffective assistance of counsel; and (4) the trial court erred in sentencing him.


OVERVIEW: Upon finding sufficient evidence to support defendant's convictions, the court then found that no error resulted from the admission of the victim's out-of-court statements because: (1) she was deceased and therefore could not offer the testimony in person; and (2) said statements constituted some of the most probative and revealing evidence offered at trial regarding defendant's threats towards the victim and the highly abusive nature of their relationship. Second, the pattern jury instruction was proper under settled Georgia case law. Finally, defendant failed to make the requisite showing with respect to each of his numerous allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. While counsel might have been deficient by failing to contend that, when the State offered evidence of defendant's prior convictions for impeachment purposes, the probative value of admitting the convictions did not substantially outweigh its prejudicial effect, defendant failed to show prejudice. Further, it was irrelevant for purposes of O.C.G.A. § 16-11-131(b) to state what felony formed the basis of the prior conviction, and specification of the underlying felony in the indictment was unnecessary.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed. But, upon the State's concession, his sentence to life in prison without parole was vacated, and the matter was remanded for the trial court to impose a legally authorized sentence.

 

203 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Granite State Outdoor Adver., Inc. v. City of Roswell


S07A1885.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


283 Ga. 417; 658 S.E.2d 587; 2008 Ga. LEXIS 246; 2008 Fulton County D. Rep. 753



March 10, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied April 11, 2008.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Granite State Adver., Inc. v. City of Roswell, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 5541 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Sign ordinance; constitutional question. Fulton Superior Court. Before Judge Russell.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed in part and vacated in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, an outdoor sign company, appealed the judgment of the trial court (Georgia), which granted defendant city's cross-motion for summary judgment and denied the company's motion for partial summary judgment. The company brought suit challenging the city's sign ordinance, after its applications to erect outdoor signs were rejected by the city.


OVERVIEW: The company contended that the city's entire sign ordinance was unconstitutional. The company had submitted three applications to construct billboard signs, each 672 square feet in area and 70 feet in height. The city rejected each application because the sign ordinance in place at the time limited off-premises signs to a maximum area of 128 square feet and 12 feet in height, and because the applications were incomplete. Subsequently, the city enacted a moratorium on the issuance of sign permits for the purpose of drafting a new sign ordinance. The company allegedly attempted to submit applications for similarly-sized signs during the moratorium, and the city did not accept any of the applications for processing. The court concluded that the trial court made no error when it held that the company had standing to contest only those provisions of the sign ordinance that had caused injury to it since Georgia had a more relaxed standard to a facial attack on an ordinance. As such, the court determined that the trial court should not have addressed the company's constitutional arguments concerning other provisions of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court vacated, in part, the trial court's rulings with regard to addressing the company's constitutional arguments concerning provisions of the ordinance that it lacked standing to challenge. Otherwise, the court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to the city.


 

204 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Mason v. Home Depot U.S.A., Inc.


S07A1486.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


283 Ga. 271; 658 S.E.2d 603; 2008 Ga. LEXIS 249; 2008 Fulton County D. Rep. 745



March 10, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: OCGA § 24-9-67.1; constitutional question. Cobb State Court. Before Judge Clayton.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed in part and vacated in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff customers appealed two orders of the trial court (Georgia), which upheld the constitutionality of O.C.G.A. § 24-9-67.1 by excising § 24-9-67.1(f) and that excluded the testimony of their two experts. Plaintiffs brought a personal injury suit against defendants, a retailer and a manufacturer, alleging that they incurred injuries while using a floor covering product made by the manufacturer and sold by the retailer.


OVERVIEW: Shortly before the case went to trial, § 24-9-67.1 was enacted, which governed the qualification of expert witnesses and the admissibility of expert testimony. Based on the new statute, and after a mistrial, the defense renewed their motion to exclude the testimony of the two experts, in response to which the customers mounted attacks on the constitutionality of § 24-9-67.1. The court held that the customers had standing to challenge the constitutionality of § 24-9-67.1 but that the trial court did not err in rejecting their equal protection challenge since they failed to show that they were similarly situated to a defendant in a criminal action as parties to civil cases are not similarly situated to those engaged in criminal prosecutions. The court also agreed that the trial court did not err by finding that § 24-9-67.1 did not violate due process but that the trial court was mistaken in declaring § 24-9-67.1(f) unconstitutional as a violation of the principle of separation of powers. However, that conclusion did not require reversal of the judgment since the trial court upheld the statute's constitutionality overall.


OUTCOME: The court vacated, in part, the trial court's order finding a subsection of the statute unconstitutional on a particular ground. Otherwise, the court affirmed the trial court.


 

205 of 777 DOCUMENTS



State v. Velazquez


S07G1012.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


283 Ga. 206; 657 S.E.2d 838; 2008 Ga. LEXIS 188; 2008 Fulton County D. Rep. 580



February 25, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Appeals of Georgia -- 283 Ga. App. 863, 643 S.E.2d 291.
Velazquez v. State, 283 Ga. App. 863, 643 S.E.2d 291, 2007 Ga. App. LEXIS 215 (2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals of Georgia vacated a rape sentence entered by the trial court, holding that defendant was incorrectly sentenced to a term of life in prison without the possibility of parole. Upon a grant of certiorari, that decision came up for review, and the instant decision followed.


OVERVIEW: In deciding the instant case, the Court of Appeals relied upon the instant court's precedent, which stated that a sentence of life without parole was authorized only in cases in which the State first sought the death penalty. Upon a review of the facts and relevant law, this reliance was proper. Specifically, under the facts involving defendant herein, the State did not supply any notice under the Uniform Rules of the Superior Courts that it intended to seek the death penalty, nor did the trial court make a finding of any aggravating circumstance under O.C.G.A. § 17-10-30. As a result, pursuant to O.C.G.A. § 17-10-32.1, the trial court was not authorized to sentence defendant to life in prison without the possibility of parole. Moreover, O.C.G.A. § 17-10-32.1 applied even after the Georgia General Assembly's 1999 inclusion of an additional potential penalty.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Court of Appeals was affirmed.

 

206 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Walker v. Hale


S07A1853, S07X1854


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


283 Ga. 131; 657 S.E.2d 227; 2008 Ga. LEXIS 130; 2008 Fulton County D. Rep. 440



February 11, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hale v. Walker, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 6016 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Habeas corpus. Richmond Superior Court. Before Judge Dickert.
Hale v. State, 274 Ga. 863, 561 S.E.2d 70, 2002 Ga. LEXIS 165 (2002)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed in Case No. S07X1854. Judgment reversed in Case No. S07A1853.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An inmate filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus against a warden. A Georgia habeas court set aside the inmate's sentence of life without parole under O.C.G.A. § 17-10-7(b)(2) but denied relief as to his convictions. The inmate and the warden each appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court stated that a felony murder charge with the underlying felony of possession of a firearm by a convicted felon had been vacated by operation of law, and no conviction on that charge had been entered. Accordingly, unlike the case relied upon by the habeas court, the trial court had not used the prior felony conviction both to support a possession conviction and to enhance the inmate's sentence. Thus, the inmate was properly sentenced to life without parole. The habeas court properly found that the inmate's prior West Virginia conviction for second-degree murder was a "serious violent felony" under O.C.G.A. § 17-10-6.1. Although the language of the West Virginia indictment, charging that the inmate did "feloniously, willfully, maliciously, deliberately and unlawfully slay, kill and murder" the victim, did not directly track the language of Georgia's murder statute, O.C.G.A. § 16-5-1, it was sufficient to show that the same offense, if committed in Georgia, would be a serious violent felony. Finally, the inmate's ineffective counsel claim was procedurally defaulted under O.C.G.A. § 9-14-48(d), and the inmate had not shown cause and prejudice to excuse the default.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the habeas court's ruling setting aside the sentence of life without parole. It affirmed the ruling denying the inmate relief on the remaining grounds.

 

207 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Cornwell v. State


S07A1559


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


283 Ga. 247; 657 S.E.2d 195; 2008 Ga. LEXIS 27; 2008 Fulton County D. Rep. 250



January 28, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied March 10, 2008.


PRIOR HISTORY: DUI, etc. Dawson Superior Court. Before Judge Gosselin.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Dawson Superior Court (Georgia) convicted defendant of driving under the influence of drugs to the extent that he was a less safe driver under O.C.G.A. § 40-6-391(a)(2), speeding, following too closely, failure to maintain lane, and failure to signal. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: An officer saw defendant's car weaving outside of its lane, closely following other vehicles, and driving in excess of 80 miles per hour. He pulled defendant over and saw that defendant's speech was slow, that he was unsteady on his feet, and that his eyes were bloodshot and glassy. After defendant performed poorly on field sobriety tests, the officer arrested him. An alcohol test was negative, but a urine test was positive for cocaine, marijuana, and six different prescription drugs. The court held that this evidence was sufficient to support the convictions. Next, the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress. When a defendant was arrested based on probable cause and the State had complied with the implied consent requirements of O.C.G.A. § 40-5-55, he could not complain that drug and alcohol testing violated the search and seizure provisions of the Fourth Amendment or the Georgia Constitution because the statute allowed for the warrantless compelled testing of bodily fluids based on the existence of probable cause, but without proof of the existence of exigent circumstances. Finally, an instruction on criminal intent, viewed in its entirety, was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

208 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Howell v. State of Ga.


S07A1292.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


283 Ga. 24; 656 S.E.2d 511; 2008 Ga. LEXIS 41; 2008 Fulton County D. Rep. 224



January 28, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Forfeiture. Irwin Superior Court. Before Judge McCorvey.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an in rem forfeiture proceeding filed pursuant to O.C.G.A. § 16-13-49, the State was granted possession of real property where law enforcement had seized 86 marijuana plants for which the property owner's father, who lived on the premises, was arrested. Thus, the property owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner alleged on appeal that the trial court erred in rejecting her claim that the forfeiture amounted to an excessive fine. The instant court disagreed, as the owner's willful blindness to her father's activities on the property in light of her knowledge of his past use of the property made her culpable. Second, although the father was subject to punishment under the criminal code for manufacturing marijuana, the owner was not subject to any punishment for permitting the property to be used criminally, other than by the instant action. Thus, the harshness of the forfeiture was not grossly disproportionate to the gravity of the offense on which it was based or to the owner's own culpability. Further, the evidence supported the trial court's rejection of her innocent-owner defense, as her serial conveyances of the property by way of gift deeds and of her lack of control over the property supported a finding that she held the property for her father, the person whose conduct gave rise to the forfeiture. Finally, as the forfeiture was not made under the power of eminent domain, but was nevertheless lawful, no Takings Clause issue was presented.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

209 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Mann v. Ga. Dep't of Corr.


S07A1043.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 754; 653 S.E.2d 740; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 849; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 3615



November 21, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied December 13, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: OCGA § 42-1-15; constitutional question. Clayton Superior Court. Before Judge Simmons.
Mann v. State, 278 Ga. 442, 603 S.E.2d 283, 2004 Ga. LEXIS 814 (2004)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a registered sex offender, challenged the judgment of the Clayton County trial court (Georgia), which denied his request for declaratory relief in regard to a residency and work restriction upon him, pursuant to O.C.G.A. § 42-1-15(a) and (b)(1) (2006). Appellant brought the declaratory suit against appellee, the Georgia Department of Corrections and others, asserting that § 42-1-15 caused a constitutional taking of his property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant moved from his parents' home, got married in 2003, and purchased a home with his wife. In 2004, appellant became the half owner and day-to-day operator of a restaurant. It was uncontroverted that neither the home nor the business were located within 1,000 feet of any child care facility, church, school, or other area where minors congregated at the time they were acquired by appellant. However, child care facilities located themselves within 1,000 feet of both appellant's home and his business and his probation officer demanded that he quit the premises of his business and remove himself from his home upon penalty of arrest and revocation of his probation. The court held that § 42-1-15(a) was unconstitutional with regard to appellant's home as it permitted the regulatory taking of his property without just and adequate compensation. However, the court found no such taking occurred with regard to appellant's business as there was no prohibition on appellant owning an interest in such business and no necessity was shown that he had to physically work in the restaurant as opposed to being able to hire others and do accounting work from his home.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, in part, the trial court's ruling denying appellant's request for declaratory relief in regard to the residency restriction and affirmed, in part, the trial court's ruling denying his request for declaratory relief in regard to the business.

 

210 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Nathans v. Diamond


S07A0738


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 804; 654 S.E.2d 121; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 857; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 3609



November 21, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied December 14, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: OCGA § 24-9-67.1; constitutional question. Fulton State Court. Before Judge Forsling.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a patient and his wife, appealed a decision of a trial court (Georgia), which granted summary judgment to appellees, a doctor and a medical group, in appellants medical malpractice action.


OVERVIEW: Summary judgment was granted based on a ruling that under O.C.G.A. § 24-9-67.1(c), appellants' expert was not qualified to give an opinion in the case. The supreme court affirmed. The doctor, an otolaryngologist, was alleged to have deviated from the standard of care regarding the informed consent given to the patient for a sleep apnea procedure. Under the statute, the expert had to be qualified in that specialty area by having actively practiced in the area with sufficient frequency to establish an appropriate level of knowledge in performing the procedure. Although the expert's affidavit stated that he performed surgeries, he did not state that he had performed the sleep apnea procedure or that he had obtained informed consents for similar surgeries. His statement that he was familiar with the standard of conduct required in similar cases was insufficient to satisfy the statute's requirements. The argument that § 24-9-67.1 affected appellants' substantive rights and was unconstitutionally applied to their case as the patient's injuries arose before the effective date of the statute was rejected. The statute was procedural in nature and was properly applied retroactively.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.


 

211 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Kaminer v. Canas


S07G0489, S07G0566, S07G0578, S07G0587.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 830; 653 S.E.2d 691; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 790; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 3272



October 29, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied December 14, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Appeals of Georgia -- 282 Ga. App. 764, 639 S.E.2d 494.
Canas v. Al-Jabi, 282 Ga. App. 764, 639 S.E.2d 494, 2006 Ga. App. LEXIS 1431 (2006)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certiorari to defendants, two physicians, a university, and a health corporation, to challenge the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Georgia), which affirmed a trial court decision concluding that plaintiff patient's medical malpractice suit based on misdiagnosis was not barred by the statute of limitations and statute of repose since additional or significantly increased symptoms of the same misdiagnosed disease were presented.


OVERVIEW: In 1984, the patient was born with a rare heart defect. At the age of two months, he underwent surgery wherein he received blood transfusions. The two physicians treated the patient and, although he displayed signs of pediatric AIDS, neither of the physicians ever arrived at that diagnosis and attributed the symptoms to the heart condition. In 2001, the patient was finally tested for HIV and diagnosed with AIDS. His condition was the result of the blood transfusions he received as an infant. The trial court granted summary judgment to defendants on all claims where the alleged negligent or wrongful act or omission occurred more than five years before the date on which the action was brought but denied summary judgment on all medical malpractice claims where the injury occurred within two years of the date the action was filed and the negligent or wrongful act or omission that caused the injury occurred within five years of the date the action was filed. The court found error, concluding that the lower courts' holdings adopted a variant of the previously rejected continuing treatment doctrine and presented a reinterpretation of the term injury set forth in O.C.G.A. § 9-3-71(a).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court. The court recognized the harsh result of its decision, which barred the patient's suit, but noted that it was for the Georgia Legislature, not the court, to make any change.


 

212 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Jones v. State


S07A0573


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 784; 653 S.E.2d 456; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 795; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 3306



October 29, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied December 14, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: Murder. Gordon Superior Court. Before Judge Nelson.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part and case remanded with direction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the trial court (Georgia), which denied his motion to suppress the evidence found at his residence and denied his motion to bar imposition of the death penalty or a life sentence without parole because the indictment did not allege the statutory aggravating circumstances. Defendant had pled guilty to four counts of murder and 18 related crimes. The State gave notice of its intent to seek the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Armed with an arrest warrant but without a search warrant, a probation officer, accompanied by several law enforcement officers, searched defendant's apartment for approximately 46 minutes. The court determined that the search was unlawful insofar as it exceeded a plain view search incident to an attempted arrest of defendant in his apartment. The court noted that the State failed to show any exigent circumstances justifying the warrantless search. Further, despite a probationer's rights under the Fourth Amendment being limited, defendant was in no way on notice that he would be subject to any warrantless searches of his residence. However, considering O.C.G.A. § 42-8-38(a), the arrest of defendant was lawful. The court further held that under Georgia law, statutory aggravating circumstances were sentencing factors rather than elements of death eligible murder, therefore, no error occurred by not including such factors in the indictment. Lastly, the court agreed with the trial court's decision upholding the constitutionality of the amended criminal discovery procedures set forth in O.C.G.A. § 17-16-1 et seq.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, in part, with regard to the denial of defendant's motion to suppress and remanded the case to the trial court regarding the motion to suppress only. Otherwise, the court affirmed the trial court's order.

 

213 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Humphrey v. Wilson


S07A1481, S07A1606


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 520; 652 S.E.2d 501; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 774; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 3278



October 26, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
As Corrected, October 26, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: Case No. S07A1481. Habeas corpus. Monroe Superior Court. Before Judge Wilson.

Case No. S07A1606. Habeas corpus. Douglas Superior Court. Before Judge Emerson.
Wilson v. State, 279 Ga. App. 459, 631 S.E.2d 391, 2006 Ga. App. LEXIS 483 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part and case remanded with direction in Case No. S07A1481. Judgment affirmed in Case No. S07A1606.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two appeals, appellant warden challenged the grant of habeas corpus relief to appellee inmate by the Monroe Superior Court (Georgia), which resulted in the inmate's conviction for aggravated child molestation being reduced to a misdemeanor. In the second appeal, the inmate challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Douglas County (Georgia), which denied his motion for release on bail during the pendency of the warden's appeal.


OVERVIEW: The court agreed with the habeas court's ruling that the inmate's sentence of 10 years in prison for having consensual oral sex with a 15-year-old girl when he was only 17 constituted cruel and unusual punishment in light of O.C.G.A. § 16-6-4 (2006), which substituted misdemeanor punishment for his conduct in place of the felony conviction of 10 years in prison with no possibility of probation or parole. The court acknowledged the 2006 amendment represented a shift in the Georgia General Assembly's view of the gravity of oral sex between two willing teenage participants and relieved such teenage offenders from registering as a sex offender. However, the court did find the habeas court erred in convicting the inmate for the misdemeanor crime, which did not exist when the conduct in question occurred. As such, the case required remand in order for the inmate's conviction and sentence to be reversed and for his discharge from custody. The court upheld the denial of a motion for release on bail, as O.C.G.A. § 17-6-1(g) prohibited bail on appeal for persons convicted of aggravated child molestation and, therefore, the inmate was not entitled to bail pending the warden's appeal.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the habeas court ruling that the inmate's sentence was cruel and unusual punishment was affirmed in part and the case was remanded to the habeas court with direction to reverse the inmate's conviction and sentence and to discharge him from custody. The denial of the inmate's motion for release on bail was affirmed.

 

214 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Quarterman v. State


S07A0659.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 383; 651 S.E.2d 32; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 580; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 2921



September 24, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: OCGA § 24-4-60; constitutional question. Fulton Superior Court. Before Judge Baxter.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted defendant's application for interlocutory appeal from the ruling of a trial court (Georgia), upholding the constitutionality of O.C.G.A. § 24-4-60, which required any person convicted of a felony and incarcerated in a State correctional facility to provide a sample for DNA analysis to determine the identification characteristics specific to the person. Defendant was indicted on charges of rape and statutory rape.


OVERVIEW: In 2005, while defendant was incarcerated in the State prison system for a felony drug conviction, a DNA sample was collected from him pursuant to § 24-4-60(b). The profile resulting from the DNA analysis was filed in the DNA database and was matched to DNA gathered from an underage female who had reported a sexual assault in June 2002. After a fresh sample of defendant's DNA was obtained by search warrant and typed, the report reflected that the DNA gathered from the 2002 victim originated from either defendant or his identical twin. The State then indicted defendant on the charges, which gave rise to his motions to suppress evidence and quash the indictment based on constitutional challenges to § 24-4-60. Defendant contended primarily that § 24-4-60 denied him equal protection of the law because it required DNA samples only from convicted felons, thereby treating such persons differently than persons convicted of misdemeanors who were incarcerated. The court found no validity to defendant's argument and held that § 24-4-60 rationally related to the legitimate State interest it was intended to promote and did not violate equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling of the trial court requiring defendant to provide the DNA sample.

 

215 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Hawthorn Suites Golf Resorts, LLC v. Feneck


S07A0704.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 554; 651 S.E.2d 664; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 590; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 2902



September 24, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied October 29, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: OCGA § 9-10-31.1 (a); constitutional question. Fulton Superior Court. Before Judge Bedford.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Delaware company brought an action against a consultant in which the company alleged wrongdoing on the consultant's part involving the management of resort property in Louisiana. A Georgia trial court dismissed the case based on forum non conveniens under O.C.G.A. § 9-10-31.1(a). The company appealed.


OVERVIEW: The consultant was living in Georgia when the company hired him. After he began managing property in Louisiana, he moved to that state to be in close proximity to it, though at the time the lawsuit was filed, he continued to own a home in Georgia. The court held that § 9-10-31.1(a) did not violate Ga. Const. art. VI, § IV, para. I. It did not automatically divest a superior court of its jurisdiction; rather, it provided directive regarding the proper venue of a given case only after the superior court exercised initial jurisdiction over the case and had exercised its discretion to determine whether the action would be more properly heard in a forum outside the state. Next, the trial court did not err in finding that Louisiana was a more convenient forum. The relative ease of access to sources of proof favored Louisiana; the witnesses could more easily be compelled to testify there; any premises to be viewed were in Louisiana; the company would not be inconvenienced by traveling to Louisiana, while the consultant would be inconvenienced by traveling to Georgia; a Georgia court would have difficulty in administering the case; and Georgia's interest in the matter was insignificant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.


 

216 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Catoosa County v. R.N. Talley Props., LLC


S07A0884.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 373; 651 S.E.2d 7; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 524; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 2277



July 13, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied September 24, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: Mandamus. Catoosa Superior Court. Before Judge Wood.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Georgia trial court held that two county provisions, challenged by appellees, a lessor and its lessee, were unconstitutionally vague in that they contained insufficient objective standards and guidelines to satisfy due process, and gave appellants, the county, its board of commissioners (board), and its chief building officer, absolute and uncontrolled discretion to decide what uses required a special use permit. The instant appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: The county, its board, and its chief building officer (officer) alleged on appeal that since the instant action was not a First Amendment case, the lessor and the lessees could not attack the constitutionality of the ordinances for facial vagueness, but rather were required to make an "as applied" challenge, for which exhaustion of administrative remedies was necessary. The instant court first held that the lessor and the lessee had not preserved an "as applied" challenge because they have never made a constitutional attack on the ordinances as applied to their property. Moreover, the trial court did not rule on that issue. Further, the lessor and the lessee did not seek a special use permit, and a building permit was not actually granted or denied. Therefore, regardless of whether they would otherwise have had a viable facial challenge to the ordinances, they lacked standing to make a constitutional attack thereon. Any remaining enumerations of error were held to be moot.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.


 

217 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Muhammad v. State


S07A0065.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 247; 647 S.E.2d 560; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 489; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 2072



June 29, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Decision reached on appeal by Muhammad v. State, 2010 Ga. LEXIS 110 (Ga., Feb. 1, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Murder. Cobb Superior Court. Before Judge Schuster.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted on charges for which the death penalty was sought. A Georgia trial court denied her motions regarding the constitutionality of the Criminal Procedure Discovery Act, O.C.G.A. § 17-16-1 et seq. Defendant sought interim review, arguing that O.C.G.A. § 17-16-4 violated her rights against self-incrimination and to effective assistance of counsel under the Fifth and Sixth Amendments and Ga. Const. art. I, § I, paras. XVI and XIV.


OVERVIEW: With regard to assistance of counsel, the court stated that under the statute, counsel could freely investigate for mitigating evidence, knowing that the identity of any potentially harmful witness resulting from that investigation need only be produced to the State in reciprocal discovery should the defense decide to call that witness at the presentence hearing. As for the requirement that a defendant disclose the witnesses she planned to call at the presentence hearing, such a provision was proper where, as here, the disclosure was limited to the nonprivileged statements related to the subject matter of the testimony of the witnesses and in a defendant's custody, possession, or control, and conditioned upon the defendant's intent to call the witnesses at the presentence hearing. Next, disclosure of the list of witnesses a defendant intended to call was personal to the defendant and thus could implicate the self-incrimination privilege. A trial court could exercise its discretion to specify the time, place, and manner of making the discovery and to enter such orders as seemed just under the circumstances under O.C.G.A. § 17-16-6 when such self-incrimination concerns arose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

218 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Fulton County v. Galberaith


S07A0032.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 314; 647 S.E.2d 24; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 467; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 1950



June 25, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied July 26, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: Sign ordinances; constitutional question. Fulton Superior Court. Before Judge Manis.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the application for discretionary appeal filed by appellants, a county, its zoning appeals board, and the board's individual members, challenging the judgment of the Fulton County Superior Court (Georgia), which held that several sections of the county's sign ordinance were unconstitutional. Appellees, an individual and his outdoor sign company, had challenged the denial of their applications to place outdoor signs on two sites.


OVERVIEW: The sign ordinance at issue prohibited off-premise signs in commercially-zoned areas. Appellees challenged the ordinance, contending that the prohibition against off-premise signs in commercially-zoned areas was an unconstitutional violation of free speech. The trial court, after determining that several sections of the sign ordinance were unconstitutional, remanded the case to the board for reconsideration of appellees' applications. The court stated that its jurisdiction in the case was necessarily limited to determining whether the trial court correctly ruled that the ordinance violated the First Amendment. The court found that the ordinance defined a billboard very broadly, so as to include any sign which advertised services, merchandise, entertainment, or information and, likewise, had a very broad definition of a sign. The ordinance, by outright banning all signs and then allowing which ones will be permitted on a case-by-case basis violated the scope and structure requirements of the First Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which held that several sections of the county's sign ordinance prohibiting off-premises signs in commercially-zoned areas was unconstitutional.


 

219 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Wagner v. State


S07A0002.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 149; 646 S.E.2d 676; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 428; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 1814



June 11, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Murder, etc. Bibb Superior Court. Before Judge Wilcox.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed in part, affirmed in part with direction and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted defendant's application for interim review of the indictment charging defendant with murder, felony murder, and concealment of a death. The State had given notice of its intent to seek the death penalty. The trial court had previously overruled defendant's demurrer to the felony murder count.


OVERVIEW: Count two of defendant's indictment attempted to charge felony murder, but it included the phrase, intentionally and with malice aforethought, which should appear in a malice murder charge, not a felony murder charge. The State could have charged malice and felony murder in the alternative in the same count but did not. Since the charge against defendant mixed those elements rather than charged them in the alternative, the court held that the felony murder count required quashing. The court found that the fact that the indictment did not state that the victim was a human being was not a material defect and merely required correction. Further, the fact that it was possible that co-defendant committed the murder for the purpose of receiving money, rather than defendant, did not cause the notice of intent to seek the death penalty to be in error and the State merely had to re-notify defendant regarding the statutory aggravating circumstance using the language of O.C.G.A. § 17-10-30(b)(4). The trial court also did not err by refusing to compel the District Attorney's testimony about his decisions to seek the death penalty as such as decision was within the prosecutor's discretion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's overruling a demurrer to the felony murder count of the indictment, directed the State to re-notify defendant as to the aggravating factor asserted against her as well as directed the State to add in the indictment that the murder victim was a human being. Otherwise, the court affirmed on all other issues raised.

 

220 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Perdue v. Lake


S07A0525.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 348; 647 S.E.2d 6; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 433; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 1810



June 11, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied July 27, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: 2006 Photo ID Act; constitutional question. Fulton Superior Court. Before Judge Westmoreland.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment vacated and case remanded with direction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff prospective voter brought an action against appellants, the Georgia State Election Board and the state governor, in which she challenged the 2006 Photo-ID Act. A Georgia trial court held that the Act violated the Georgia Constitution. Appellants challenged the trial court's decision.


OVERVIEW: The court held that at the time she filed the complaint, plaintiff lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the Act. A plaintiff had to show that she had been harmed at the time she filed a complaint alleging a constitutional violation. Here, on the date she filed the complaint, plaintiff could have voted in person under O.C.G.A. § 21-2-417(c) without the need to show a photo identification (ID) card. She had not contended that she lacked any of the forms of non-photo identification allowed to be shown by first-time voters. Accordingly, she had not been harmed by the Act. Furthermore, at the time she filed her complaint, plaintiff had a form of photo ID acceptable under § 21-2-417(a)(2). She had a photo ID card issued by a transit authority, which plaintiff conceded was an entity of the State of Georgia and which the authority had issued to comply with the Americans with Disabilities Act. Accordingly, as plaintiff lacked standing, the determination that the Act was unconstitutional had to be vacated.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment. It remanded the case with the direction that it be dismissed.


 

221 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Fowler Props. v. Dowland


S07A0342, S07A0777.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 76; 646 S.E.2d 197; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 402; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 1698



June 4, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
OCGA § 9-11-68; constitutional question. Cherokee Superior Court. Before Judge Jordan, pro hac vice.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff pedestrian brought a personal injury suit against defendant parking lot owners. She alleged that she had slipped and fallen on ice in the lot. A jury in a Georgia trial court returned a verdict in favor of defendants. The trial court denied plaintiff's motion for a new trial and denied defendants' request for attorney fees under O.C.G.A. § 9-11-68, which it held to be unconstitutional. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: Section 9-11-68 became effective while the parties' litigation was pending. The court held that the statute violated Ga. Const. art. I, § I, para. X as a retrospective application of law that altered vested rights. The statute did not merely prescribe methods of enforcing rights and obligations, but affected the rights of parties by imposing an additional obligation to pay an opposing party's attorney fees when a final judgment did not meet a certain amount or was one of no liability. Next, the jury had asked the trial court whether plaintiff had said that she had slipped because of ice. Plaintiff's testimony was lengthy, and she had vacillated throughout as to the cause of her fall and the conditions on the ground. Thus, the trial court properly refused to parse plaintiff's testimony or to have the tape of her testimony played in its entirety to the jury. Finally, the trial court properly refused to give a proposed jury instruction as to an accumulation of naturally occurring ice. Both the charge requested and the charge given covered a proprietor's duty to exercise ordinary care, including the duty to inspect the premises for dangerous conditions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments.

223 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Allen v. Wright


S06G2018


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 9; 644 S.E.2d 814; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 343; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 1457



May 14, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari to the Court of Appeals of Georgia -- 280 Ga. App. 554, 634 S.E.2d 518.
Allen v. Wright, 280 Ga. App. 554, 634 S.E.2d 518, 2006 Ga. App. LEXIS 893 (2006)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a doctor and others, were granted certiorari to review the decision of the Court of Appeals (Georgia), which affirmed a trial court order denying appellants' motion to dismiss the medical malpractice suit filed by appellee patient. The appellate court agreed with the trial court that O.C.G.A. § 9-11-9.2 was preempted by the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act of 1996 (HIPAA).


OVERVIEW: Appellee had executed an authorization to release her medical records and filed the same contemporaneously with her complaint. Appellants had moved to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the authorization did not satisfy the requirements of § 9-11-9.2 by failing to authorize their attorneys to communicate with appellee's treating physicians outside the presence of and without prior notification to her lawyer, even though the statute did not expressly provide that a plaintiff's requisite authorization must grant such ex parte discovery rights to the defendant. The trial court denied the motion to dismiss, holding that § 9-11-9.2 was preempted by HIPAA. The preemption issue had already been decided by the appellate court in a separate suit, but since the preemption question was an issue of first impression and certiorari had not been sought in that other suit, the court granted appellants' petition. The court upheld the ruling that HIPAA preempted § 9-11-9.2 since § 9-11-9.2 did not sufficiently comply with the HIPAA requirement of notice of the right to revoke and was less stringent than the requirements mandated by the federal law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.

Maxwell v. State


S07A0020


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 22; 644 S.E.2d 822; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 345; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 1471



May 14, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Murder. Fulton Superior Court. Before Judge Schwall.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the trial court (Georgia), which convicted appellant for felony murder and other crimes stemming from an incident in which he drove his car into a crowd, hitting and killing a victim.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, appellant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence to support his convictions, but the trial court found that sufficient evidence existed for a rational trier of fact to have found appellant guilty beyond a reasonable doubt of all the crimes for which he was convicted, particularly since appellant's contention that O.C.G.A. § 40-6-395 only applied to officers on foot would have rendered the phrase "pursuing police vehicle" in the first sentence of § 40-6-395 meaningless, with a court being prohibited from interpreting a statute in such a manner. The trial court completely failed to question the juror with regard to his ability to lay aside his prejudices and personal experiences and fairly and impartially decide the case on the evidence presented at trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant's convictions and remanded the case to the trial court for a new trial.

 

225 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Glenn v. State


S07A0079. S07A0080.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


282 Ga. 27; 644 S.E.2d 826; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 346; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 1467; 29 A.L.R.6th 793



May 14, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
OCGA § 16-17-1 et seq.; constitutional question. Decatur Superior Court. Before Judge Cato.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Decatur Superior Court (Georgia) convicted both defendants of multiple counts of violating O.C.G.A. § 16-17-2, which prohibited the making of "payday loans," which were defined as loans of $ 3,000 or less with illegal interest rates. Both defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Both defendants challenged the constitutionality of O.C.G.A. § 16-17-1 et seq., alleging that the trial court erred by rejecting their equal protection and vagueness challenges. The appeals court disagreed, instead agreeing with the State, that defendants were not similarly situated with the out-of-state banks designated in O.C.G.A. § 16-17-2(a)(3). Both defendants, as in-state lenders, were subject to Georgia statutes regulating or restricting high interest rates on loans, whereas the out-of-state banks were not. In light of the protected status of out-of-state banks under federal law, the Georgia Legislature had a rational basis for creating a class based on those in-state payday lenders who were subject to state regulation. Thus, the classification bore an obvious and direct relation to the legitimate purposes set forth in O.C.G.A. § 16-17-1(c) and (d). Further, the court found that the prohibition against payday loans, under O.C.G.A. § 16-17-1 et seq., in whatever form transacted, was sufficiently definite to satisfy due process standards.


OUTCOME: The convictions entered against both defendants were affirmed.

 

226 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Marathon Inv. Corp. v. Spinkston


S07A0523.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 888; 644 S.E.2d 133; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 306; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 1373



April 24, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Title to land. Fulton Superior Court. Before Judge Dempsey.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a quiet title action filed by the successful bidder at a tax sale against two trustees, in their representative capacities for the subject property, a special master found in the trustees' favor on the issue that the property was tax-exempt, making the tax sale void or voidable. A Georgia trial court approved and adopted the special master's report, vesting title in the trustees and declaring the tax sale void. The bidder appealed.


OVERVIEW: After a review of the record, it was undisputed that the property at issue was exempt from taxes. But, the trustees never received notice of the tax sale. Moreover, no past or present member of the congregation ever lived at the address which the tax notices were sent, and the special master was authorized to so find. Thus, because the trustees were never given the required notice, their due process rights were violated by the tax sale and the deed to the bidder resulting from that unconstitutional sale was, therefore, void. Second, the exception to the tender requirement applied, as taxes were not due at the time the property was sold because of its tax-exempt status.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

In the Interest of J.R.R.


S07A0085.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 662; 641 S.E.2d 526; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 170; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 473



February 26, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Termination of guardianship; constitutional question. Cherokee Probate Court. Before Judge McVay.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Georgia probate court struck two provisions in the recently revised guardianship code, O.C.G.A. § 29-1-1 et seq., as unconstitutional. In the same order, it granted a petition to terminate a guardianship awarded to appellant grandmother, prompting her appeal. The Court of Appeals then transferred the same to the instant court on jurisdictional grounds.


OVERVIEW: Upon a review of the facts, the instant court found that the probate court distinctly passed on the constitutionality of provisions of the guardianship statute. However, the petition to terminate the guardianship that was filed in the probate court did not make any constitutional challenge. Indeed, no constitutional question was raised in the pleadings or any other portion of the record. Even assuming that a constitutional question had been asserted, it was not properly raised by mere oral argument. As a result, the probate court was without the authority to declare an a law unconstitutional in the absence of a proper attack upon it.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Merry v. Williams


S06A1690.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 571; 642 S.E.2d 46; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 126; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 268



February 5, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Mandamus. Richmond Superior Court. Before Judge Overstreet.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part, and case remanded with direction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a citizen and taxpayer of a county, challenged the judgment of the trial court (Georgia), which dismissed appellant's petition for declaratory relief and denied his writs for mandamus and quo warranto. Appellant brought suit against defendants, the county commission, a mayor, and each individual commissioner, seeking primarily to hold a new election for mayor pro tempore for 2006. A commissioner cross-claimed for declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: In two elections held the same night, one commissioner received five yes votes and four no votes, and another commissioner received four yes votes and five no votes for the mayor pro tempore position. One commissioner abstained from each vote. Because the year 2006 had ended, the court agreed that all of appellant's claims, but for declaratory relief, were rendered moot. Since the mayor's method of counting votes remained, the court held that the declaratory judgment action survived and was not moot. The court held that the Consolidation Act, 1997 Ga. Laws 4690, 4691 and 1995 Ga. Laws 3648, 3650, § 1, required that abstentions by commissioners not be counted as affirmative votes and that the rules of procedure to be followed by appellees provided that they not be counted as negative votes. In that regard, the rules were not inconsistent with the Consolidation Act. As a result, under the current law applicable to the county, abstentions by commissioners must not be counted as either affirmative or negative votes, therefore, appellant was not entitled to declaratory relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, in part, that portion of the judgment that dismissed the declaratory judgment action and remanded the case to the trial court for the entry of an order denying such relief to appellant. Otherwise, the court affirmed the judgment.

Stinski v. State


S06A1455.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 783; 642 S.E.2d 1; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 128; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 289



February 5, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied March 27, 2007.
Related proceeding at O'Kelley v. State, 284 Ga. 758, 670 S.E.2d 388, 2008 Ga. LEXIS 869 (2008)
Decision reached on appeal by Stinski v. State, 2010 Ga. LEXIS 186 (Ga., Mar. 1, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Murder, etc. Chatham Superior Court. Before Judge Bass.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted on malice murder, burglary, arson, entering an automobile, cruelty to children, and possession of a controlled substance. The State sought the death penalty. A Georgia trial court denied certain pretrial motions by defendant. The present court granted interim review.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the trial court properly denied a motion to suppress a tote bag on the ground that defendant lacked standing to seek suppression of it. Defendant had presented no admissible testimony as to whether he was aggrieved by an unlawful search and seizure under O.C.G.A. § 17-5-30(a). Next, defendant's custodial statements were not inadmissible under O.C.G.A. § 24-3-50. It was not improper for police to encourage a suspect to help himself by telling the truth. Furthermore, regardless of whether defendant's warrantless arrest inside his home was illegal, the statements he made later outside the home during his custodial interrogation did not have to be suppressed on federal constitutional grounds. Next, after defendant elected to participate in the criminal discovery procedure under O.C.G.A. § 17-16-1 et seq., the statutes had been amended. Defendant's previous election to participate was binding upon him. The amended procedure was not unconstitutional. It imposed reciprocal discovery burdens upon the State, and any difference in the scope of mitigating evidence and the scope of nonstatutory aggravating evidence was too minimal to be of constitutional significance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's orders.

 

230 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Banta v. State


S06A2032.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 615; 642 S.E.2d 51; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 134; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 275



February 5, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration denied February 26, 2007. As Corrected February 26, 2007.
Related proceeding at Banta v. State, 282 Ga. 392, 651 S.E.2d 21, 2007 Ga. LEXIS 588 (Ga., Sept. 24, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Making a false statement; constitutional question. Whitfield Superior Court. Before Judge Boyett.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Georgia trial court convicted defendant of filing a false statement to a government agency, a felony, contrary to O.C.G.A. § 16-10-20. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction. But, the appeals court disagreed with his first contention that O.C.G.A. § 16-10-20 was unconstitutionally vague under Ga. Const. art. I, § I, para. I, as the statute gave him ample notice that the conduct of which he was accused was prohibited. The statute also provided sufficient objective standards to those who were charged with enforcing it. Defendant's act was criminal when he made his false statement, without regard to the result of that act. The fact that application of the statute's standards sometimes required an assessment of the surrounding circumstances to determine if the statute was violated did not render it unconstitutional. Second, the court rejected defendant's claim that under the rule of lenity, his crime could only be considered a misdemeanor, because the acts alleged met the definition of misdemeanor obstruction of a police officer, as O.C.G.A. §§ 16-10-24 and 16-10-20 did not define the same offense, did not address the same criminal conduct, and there was no ambiguity created by different punishments being set forth for the same crime. Hence, the rule of lenity did not apply.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

231 of 777 DOCUMENTS



State v. Henderson


S06A2040. S06A2041.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 623; 641 S.E.2d 515; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 40; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 189



January 22, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration denied February 26, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: Jurisdiction. Floyd Superior Court. Before Judge Colston.



DISPOSITION: Judgement reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Floyd County Superior Court (Georgia) denied the State's motion to stay a transfer of its prosecution of appellee juveniles in the superior court, finding that, although O.C.G.A. § 15-11-28(b)(1) granted it concurrent jurisdiction over the cases before it, the statute was unconstitutional in that it violated separation of powers, equal protection, and due process, the State's application for interlocutory appeal was granted.


OVERVIEW: Upon a review of the record, the instant court noted that neither the superior court nor any of the parties disputed that the superior court had at least concurrent jurisdiction over the cases at hand. Hence, once the State elected to invoke the superior court's jurisdiction by pursuing the prosecution of the juveniles in superior court, the court, acting on its own volition, did not have the authority to transfer the cases to juvenile court prior to indictment. Second, while the superior court concluded that O.C.G.A. § 15-11-28(b)(1) violated due process, equal protection, and separation of powers by failing to provide for judicial review of a prosecutor's decision to charge minors in superior court rather than juvenile court, the instant court previously rejected similar arguments, finding that O.C.G.A. § 15-11-28(b)(1) was constitutional. Hence, the court's finding as to this issue was also erroneous.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

232 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Hernandez v. State


S06A1712.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 559; 639 S.E.2d 473; 2007 Ga. LEXIS 2; 2007 Fulton County D. Rep. 93



January 8, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration denied February 5, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY: OCGA § 16-9-121; constitutional question. Madison Superior Court. Before Judge Bailey.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed after he was convicted in a Georgia trial court on a charge of identity fraud. Defendant contended, inter alia, that the identity fraud statute, O.C.G.A. § 16-9-121, was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant misappropriated the victim's social security number and used it in obtaining a job. His wages were reported to the Internal Revenue Service (IRS) under the victim's name. The victim discovered the identify theft after the IRS informed him he owed back taxes. The supreme court affirmed. Contrary to defendant's assertion, he had in fact "accessed a resource" of the victim as required by § 16-9-121. By using the victim's social security number to obtain a job, defendant accessed the victim's IRS account and thereby the United States Treasury (a "resource" for purposes of O.C.G.A. § 16-9-120(5)(F)) and caused harm to victim by attempting to make him responsible for defendant's tax debt. O.C.G.A. § 16-9-121 explicitly prohibited the improper access of another's account at a financial institution. The plain language of O.C.G.A. §§ 16-9-120 and 121 placed defendant on notice that his actions were illegal. The statute, therefore, was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant. Finally, nothing in the federal law explicitly overrode O.C.G.A. § 16-9-121, and as it did not conflict with the operation or enforcement of federal law, it was not preempted 8 U.S.C.S. § 1324c.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

 

233 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Atlanta Taxicab Co. Owners Ass'n v. City of Atlanta


S06A0923.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 342; 638 S.E.2d 307; 2006 Ga. LEXIS 1030; 2006 Fulton County D. Rep. 3705



November 30, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] City taxicab regulations; constitutional question. Fulton Superior Court. Before Judge Tusan.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An association of taxicab owners challenged an order of the trial court (Georgia) that granted a motion for summary judgment filed by a city in the association's action seeking damages and a declaration that certain sections of Atlanta, Ga., City Code ch. 162, under which the city regulated its taxicab industry, were unconstitutional. The association's members held certificates of public necessity and convenience (CPNC) issued under ch. 162.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court reversed the trial court judgment in part. Establishment of an administrative procedure to enforce Atlanta, Ga., City Code ch. 162 was within the city's power under its charter and did not improperly divest the municipal court of jurisdiction. The one-year residency requirement for issuance of a CPNC in Atlanta, Ga., City Code § 162-57(a)(3) violated the Commerce Clause. The association had standing to raise the challenge, and the city advanced no legitimate local purpose served by the residency requirement. Fines and penalties imposed on the association's members for their drivers' regulatory violations did not violate substantive due process by creating vicarious criminal liability. To the extent that City of Atlanta v. Fuller, 164 S.E.2d 364 (1968) held that the notice required by O.C.G.A. § 36-33-5 could be accomplished by amending a complaint, it was overruled. The association, however, properly amended its complaint to add a claim that the city's notice procedures for violations violated due process after serving notice under § 36-33-5. The appellate court did not rule on the sufficiency of the notice because the trial court had not ruled on that issue.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court judgment insofar as it ruled that the one-year residency requirement of Atlanta, Ga., City Code § 162-57(a)(3) was constitutional and insofar as it dismissed the association's claim that the city's notice procedures violated procedural due process on the ground that the association gave improper ante-litem notice of the claim. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.


 

234 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Briggs v. State


S06A1146.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 329; 638 S.E.2d 292; 2006 Ga. LEXIS 1025; Copy. L. Rep. (CCH) P29,291; 2006 Fulton County D. Rep. 3716; 80 U.S.P.Q.2D (BNA) 1938



November 29, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected, December 15, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY: OCGA § 16-8-60 (b); constitutional question. Clarke Superior Court. Before Judge Jones.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon charging defendant with violating O.C.G.A. § 16-8-60(b), defendant attacked the statute and moved to dismiss the indictment on constitutional and federal preemption grounds. The Georgia trial court found that the statute was neither unconstitutionally vague nor overbroad and was not preempted by federal copyright law. Defendant was thus granted interlocutory review, and this matter followed.


OVERVIEW: At issue was whether O.C.G.A. § 16-8-60(b), which criminalized the possession and distribution of sounds or images without a label in violation of copyright protection, was either (1) unconstitutionally vague or overbroad or (2) preempted by federal copyright law. The instant court said it was not. First, the statute plainly prohibited the sale, or possession for the purposes of sale, of an article that did not prominently display the name and address of the individual (or entity) who transferred the sounds to the article. Second, the statute did not impinge upon pure speech but, at most, regulated a combination of commercial conduct and speech. Third, the statute aimed to protect the public and entertainment industry from piracy and bootlegging, a legitimate governmental interest unrelated to free speech concerns. To the extent that it curtailed an artist's or transferor's desire to remain anonymous, its deterrent effect on legitimate expression was minimal. Finally, there was no federal preemption as the statute contained an extra element, specifically, labeling, which qualitatively distinguished it from federal copyright law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

235 of 777 DOCUMENTS




DaimlerChrysler v. Ferrante


S06A0902. S06A1219. S06A1221. S06A1222. S06A1223. S06A1225.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 273; 637 S.E.2d 659; 2006 Ga. LEXIS 960; 2006 Fulton County D. Rep. 3555



November 20, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Asbestos claims; constitutional question. Cobb Superior Court. Before Judge Glover, pro hac vice.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior and State Courts of Cobb County (Georgia) held that because the newly enacted asbestos claims statute, O.C.G.A. § 51-14-1 et seq., required plaintiffs to show that asbestos exposure substantially contributed to their medical condition, it unconstitutionally affected appellee employees' substantive rights by establishing a new element of their claim, which did not previously exist. Appellant employers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employers alleged that the lower courts erred by ruling that the O.C.G.A. § 51-14-1 et seq. affected substantive rights and therefore could not be applied retrospectively to claims which accrued prior to its effective date. The instant court agreed. Also, the statute could not accurately be described as merely establishing a procedural threshold for a preliminary determination of causation. Because the legislature decided to include the requirement that asbestos plaintiffs produce evidence that exposure to asbestos was a substantial contributing factor in the exposed persons' medical conditions and to place further restrictions on the form of such evidence, such demonstrated a clear intent to limit actionable asbestos claims to those situations in which a greater level of causation was shown. Hence, severance of this requirement would result in a statute that failed to correspond to the main legislative purpose, or give effect to that purpose, and the presence of a severability clause did not alter this result.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

236 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Brewer v. State of Ga.


S06A1104.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 283; 637 S.E.2d 677; 2006 Ga. LEXIS 976; 2006 Fulton County D. Rep. 3586



November 20, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Forfeiture; constitutional question. Lumpkin Superior Court. Before Judge Struble, Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Georgia trial court denied a motion to vacate an order condemning the firearms seized from appellant owner and authorizing their distribution, rejecting the owner's challenge that O.C.G.A. § 16-13-49(n) was unconstitutional. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner challenged the judgment entered against him. But, the court first found that contrary to his assertion, the publication method selected by the Georgia Legislature did not need to ensure actual notice in order for O.C.G.A. § 16-13-49(n) to comport with due process. Second, the court disagreed with the owner that O.C.G.A. § 16-13-49(n) had to provide for notice to be printed in the county's legal organ in order to satisfy due process. Pretermitting the undisputed facts that the owner was served by certified mail and notice was published in the legal organ, publication in a newspaper of general circulation was sufficient to satisfy the requirements of due process. Third, the court rejected the owner's argument that O.C.G.A. § 16-13-49(n) violated his right to bear arms under the Second Amendment to the United States Constitution. Fourth, the owner's argument under the Georgia Constitution was not raised and ruled on below, and thus, it was not addressed. Finally, 18 U.S.C.S. § 922(g)(8), cited by the owner, had absolutely no application to state civil forfeiture cases.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.


 

237 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Thomason v. State


S06A1478.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 429; 637 S.E.2d 639; 2006 Ga. LEXIS 927; 2006 Fulton County D. Rep. 3381



November 6, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration denied December 15, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY: Murder. Stephens Superior Court. Before Judge Woods.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the trial court (Georgia) of malice murder, aggravated battery, and possession of a firearm during the commission of a felony, after the denial of his motions for a mistrial and for a directed verdict. After finding aggravating circumstances existed for the murder, the jury fixed defendant's sentence at life without parole. Defendant was sentenced. Defendant's motion for a new trial was denied. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the evidence did not support the aggravated battery conviction. The appellate court held that the evidence supported the convictions. Defendant's request for more than 15 peremptory strikes was properly denied. O.C.G.A. § 16-5-1 was constitutional. The Unified Appeal Procedure was constitutional. The denial of the request to shuffle the jury was proper. The statutory aggravating factors did not have to be included in the indictment. It was not error to read the names of the grand jurors when the indictment was read. O.C.G.A. § 17-10-1.2 was constitutional. Six jurors were properly seated. Photographs of the victim's body were properly admitted. The victim's mother was properly permitted to testify to a statement made by the victim that defendant threatened to kill her. A curative instruction was properly given after an outburst by the victim's mother. A request for a jury instruction on voluntary manslaughter was properly denied. A motion for a change in venue was properly denied. Finally, a motion for recusal of the trial judge and the prosecutor was properly denied. Jurors who expressed an objection to the death penalty were properly excused.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

238 of 777 DOCUMENTS



Barnes v. City of Atlanta


S06G0162.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 256; 637 S.E.2d 4; 2006 Ga. LEXIS 831; 2006 Fulton County D. Rep. 3164



October 16, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration denied November 20, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Appeals of Georgia -- 275 Ga. App. 385.
Barnes v. City of Atlanta, 275 Ga. App. 385, 620 S.E.2d 846, 2005 Ga. App. LEXIS 975 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action by plaintiffs, class one attorneys and class two attorneys, demanding a refund of occupational taxes imposed by defendant City of Atlanta, review was sought of the Court of Appeals of Georgia's judgment affirming the finding that class one had to exhaust its administrative remedies, the recertification of class one to permit a request of tax refunds, and the holding that some refunds were barred by the limitations period.


OVERVIEW: The attorneys, seeking a tax refund under O.C.G.A. § 48-5-380, alleged that the occupation taxes imposed by the City were an unconstitutional regulation of the practice of law. The case was certified as a class action, and the attorneys were divided into two classes. Class two consisted of attorneys who had already demanded a refund, while class one consisted of attorneys who had not. The state high court determined that the City's occupation tax ordinance was unconstitutional to the extent that it included lawyers. The intermediate appellate court then issued the judgment at issue in the instant appeal. The most important factor in applying the administrative exhaustion requirement to a case involving the constitutionality of taxes was the City's need for early notice of its potential liability. The City had notice of the constitutional challenge to the validity of the occupation tax ordinance when the administrative claims and subsequent lawsuits were filed by the attorneys. Thus, the named attorneys, who had exhausted their administrative remedies, satisfied the exhaustion requirement of the class one attorneys, as the named attorneys acted on behalf of the entire class.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

239 of 777 DOCUMENTS




Brodie v. Champion


S06A1029., S06X1030.


SUPREME COURT OF GEORGIA


281 Ga. 105; 636 S.E.2d 511; 2006 Ga. LEXIS 833; 2006 Fulton County D. Rep. 3175



October 16, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Election contest. Fulton Superior Court. Before Judge Fuller, Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Georgia trial court held that O.C.G.A. § 21-2-494 was constitutional and in a separate order denied defendant incumbent's motion to dismiss. Plaintiff challenger appealed the order finding the statute constitutional, while the incumbent appealed from an order denying her motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The challenger alleged that O.C.G.A. § 21-2-494 was unconstitutional merely because it impermissibly allowed the exclusion of votes for write-in candidates and because it did not require that voters be provided with notice that write-in votes for unqualified candidates would not be counted. The instant court disagreed. The Board of Registration and Elections (Board) considered the write-in votes, as it was required to, in order to determine whether the votes had been cast for constitutionally qualified candidates. Further, no voters were disenfranchised, as there was no dispute that the write-in candidates were not eligible to serve. Third, no merit was given to the challenger's argument that O.C.G.A. § 21-2-494 was unconstitutional for failing to require that voters be given notice that write-in votes for unqualified write-in candidates would not be counted. As to the incumbent's appeal, the challenger's failure to name the Board as a party defendant did not warrant dismissal, as said failure was an amendable defect, correctable by the parties or upon the court's own motion.


OUTCOME: The judgments entered by the trial court were both affirmed.
